Re\xad searchers who study solution processes have made a distinction between algorithmic and heuristic strategies.\nAn algOrithm is a procedure  that  is guar\xad anteed to yield the solution. One type of al\xad gorithm  is a  mathematical formula. For ex\xad ample,  multiplying  the  length  of the  base of a rectangle times its height is guaranteed to  yield  the  rectangle\'s  area.  Similarly,  the formula\n$$X = \\frac { - b \\pm \\sqrt { b ^ { 2 } - 4 a c } } { }$$\nis  guaranteed  to  provide  the quadratic equation roots  of  the\n$$a X ^ { 2 } + b X + c = 0 .$$\nWe discuss mathematical problem solVing in some detail in "The Interplay Between Rep\xad resentation and  Solution"  (also see  Gallistel & Gelman, Chap. 23).\nAnother  type  of algorithm -exhaustive search - involves  checking  every  possible move.  For  example,  one  could  solve  the Tower  of  Hanoi  problem  by  exhaustively considering every possible  move  in fig\xad ure  1 4 . 2 .   Similarly,  one  could  solve  a  f our\xad letter anagram (e.g., idrb) by systematically evaluating the 24  possible permutations of the  given  letters  (the  solution  is bird). For problems  with  a  large  number  of pos\xad sible  states,  however,  exhaustive  search  is impractical  or  impossible.  For  example,  if the  task  is to find all possible solutions of a five-letter anagram  (e.g., ebrda forms bread,\nbeard,  bared, and debar), exhaustive search would require examination of 1 20 letter or\xad ders.  More strikingly,  consider  the  game  of chess (Holding, 1 985): White has 20 possible opening moves, to which black can respond in any of 20 ways. Thus! on the second turn, white may be contronted with  any of 400 possible board positions. After white\'s third move, there are 7.5 million  possible board positions; after black\'s third move, there are 225  million possible positions. For a game of average length, the number of possible posi\xad tions is approximately 1 0117.\nClearly, some method is needed to prune the  number  of possible  moves  to  be  con\xad sidered.  Such  pruning  is  necessary tor  hu\xad man solvers owing to  the  limited capacity of working memory; it is also necessary for computers when, as in chess, the number of possible states is extremely  large. Heuristics arc  problem-solving strategies  that  accom\xad plish  this  goal.  Although  heuristics  do  not guarantee solution, they are highly likely to lead to success. For example J  a good heuristic for  solving anagrams,  especially those  with five  or  more letters  (e.g., dsyha) ,  is  to con\xad sider letter pairs that commonly begin words of  the  given  length  (e.g.,  Ronning,  1 965). This  heuristic  is  useful  because,  by  defini\xad tion, most words begin with common letter pairs. Application of  this heuristic to the ex\xad ample  should  qUickly lead  to  the  solution, shady. That considering common initial let\xad ter pairs  is  a  heuristic  rather  than  an  algo\xad rithm is  nicely  illustrated by a  second  ana\xad gram, uspyr, which cannot be solved by this strategy  because  it  begins  with  an  uncom\xad mon  letter  pair  (the solution, syrup, is  the only five-letter word in  English that begins with sy; Novick & Sherman, 2004).\nA large  body of literature has  examined the  heuristics  that  people  use  to  generate problem  solutions. These problems are  useful  because  they  enable  researchers to  focus  their  attention  primarily  on  the process of generating solutions. This heuristic consists of  the following steps:\n- I . IdentifY  a  difference  between  the  cur\xad rent state and the goal (or subgoal) state.\n- 2 .   Find an operator that will remove (or re\xad due  e)  the difference.\n- p. If the operator can  be directly applied, do so, or\n- 3  b.  If  the  operator  cannot  be  directly  ap\xad plied, set a subgoal to remove the obsta\xad cle  that  is  preventing  execution  of the desired operator.\n4.  Repeat steps  1  to 3 until the problem is solved.\nWe illustrate this heuristic  with the Tower of  Hanoi  problem.  A  key  difference  be\xad tween  the  initial  state  and  the  goal  state (Figure  14.2)  is  that the  large  disk  is  on the wrong peg (step I]. The move-large-disk operator  is  required  to  remove  this  differ\xad ence  (step 2).  However,  this operator  can\xad not  be  applied  because  of the  presence  of the  medium  and small disks on  top  of the large  disk.  Theref ore,  the  solver  may  set  a subgoal to move that two-disk tower to the middle peg  (step  3b),  thereby  leaving  the right peg free for the large  disk.  A key ditÔøΩ ference  between  the  initial  state  and  this new subgoal state is that the  medium disk is on the wrong peg. Because application of the mave-medium-disk operator is blocked, the  solver  sets  another  subgoal  to  move the  small  disk  to  the  right  peg.  This  sub\xad goal can be satisfied immediately by apply\xad ing the move-small-disk operator (step p), generating  state  #3.  The  solver  then  re\xad turns to  the  previous  subgoal  - moving the tower consisting of the  small  and  medium disks to the middle peg. The differences be\xad tween the current state (#3) and the subgoal state  (#9) can be removed by applying first\nthe  move-medium-disk  operator  (yielding state # 5 )  and then the move-small-disk op\xad erator (yielding state #9). Finally, the move\xad large-disk operator is no longer blocked. The solver  takes  that  action,  moving  the  large disk to the right peg, yielding state #1 1 .  No\xad tice that the subgoals are stacked up in the order  in which they are  generated  so they pop up in the order of last in first out. Given the  first  subgoal in our  example, repeated ap\xad plication of the means-ends analysis heuris\xad tic will yield the shortest-path solution indi\xad cated by the thick grey arrows.\nThe key diff erence between hill climbing and  mean-ends  analysis  is  the  online  gen\xad eration  of subgoals  in  the  latter  heuristic. Adding new subgoals during problem solv\xad ing greatly increases the power of heuristic search.  Subgoals  provide  direction,  and  to the  extent  that  they  are  appropriate,  they can be expected to prune the space of pos\xad sible states.  Moreover, by assessing progress toward a required subgoal rather than the fi\xad nal goal, solvers may be able to make moves that otherwise seem unwise. To  take a con\xad crete example} consider the transition from state #1  to state #3  in Figure 1 4.2. Compar\xad ing the  initial  state  with  the  goal state,  we find  that  this  move  seems  unwise  because it  places  the  small  disk  on  the  bottom  of the  right  peg,  whereas  it  ultimately  needs to be  at  the  top of the tower on that peg. However,  if one compares the  initial  state with  the  solver-generated  subgoal  state  of having the medium disk on the middle peg, this is exactly where the small disk needs to go.  More  generally,  generating  subgoals  al\xad lows  solvers  to  plan  several  moves  ahead. (Duncker,  1 945,  also talked about the  im\xad portance of subgoals.)\nAs we noted in our brief historic review, means-ends analysis is the heuristic that GPS used to successfully model human problem solving across a wide variety of tasks (Ernst & Newell, 1 969; Newell & Simon, 1972). A large body of research has found that mean\xad ends analysis tends to be people's preferred solution method for novel problems that are relatively fTee of specialized content and for which  a According  to  the  Gestalt  view (e.g., Duncker, \' 94 5 ;  Maier,  \' 93 \' ;  see Ohls\xad son, \'984a, lor a reView), the solution to an insight  problem  appears  suddenly,  accom\xad panied by an "aha!" sensation,  immediately follOWing the sudden restructuring of one\'s understanding of  the problem: "The decisive points in thought-processes, the moments of sudden comprehension, of the \'Aha\',\' of  the\nnew,  are ays at  the same time moments in  which  such  a  sudden  restructuring  of the thought-material takes place" (Duncker, 1 945, p.  29)¬∑ For the nine-dot problem, one view of  the required restructuring is that the solver relaxes the constraint implied by the perceptual lorm of  the problem and realizes that  the  lines  in  fact  may  extend  past  the boundary of the imaginary square. alw\nTo test  this  view,  in  one  experiment Weisberg and Alba (1981 ) compared the per\xad lormance of contra  I subjects who were given 20 attempts to solve the nine-dot problem with  that  of other  subjects  who  received 1 0   attempts before a restructuring hint, 101lowed  by  1 0   attempts  after  the  hint.  The restructuring  hint  involved  telling  subjects that they had exhausted  all  possibilities  in\xad side the square, and so they had to go outside the  square  to  solve  the  problem. Moreover,  75% to 80% of the subjects failed to solve the problem despite the hint. Thus, restructuring, as provided by Weisberg and Alba\'s hint, appears to be necessary but not sufficient for solution. How\xad ever,  poor learners  failed  to  recognize  the structural  similarity between  the examples and the novel problems.  People\'s ability to exploit analogous solutions also depends on their domain expertise. We discuss expertise differences  in  problem representation after considering schematic knowledge. Then, in \'The Interplay Between Representation and Solution" we consider the  implications  of  ex\xad pertise differences for analogical transfer.\nGE:NERAL  SCHE:MAS IN  ME:MORY\nIn  addition  to  knowledge  of speCific  prob\xad lems  encountered  in  the  past,  solvers  also have in memory abstract schemas for types of problems,  types  of solution  procedures, and types of  representations. These schemas are  abstract  in  the sense  that  they  include information  that  is  common  to  multiple problems of a  particular type but  exclude information  that is  idiosyncratic  to  the  in\xad dividual  problems  over  which  the abstrac\xad tion has occurred.  For example,  an abstract schema for the convergence solution would specify  that  multiple,  low-intensity  forces converge from different directions on a cen\xad tral target, but it would not specify that the forces  are soldiers  (or rays) or that the tar\xad get  is  a fortress  (or  a tumor).  A  number of studies have shown thatschemas for solution procedures  can  be  induced  by  comparing two or more analogous problems (with their solutions) or  by  successfully  solVing  one problem  by  analogy  to  another  (solved) problem, and such schema induction in turn facilitates understanding and solution of  sub\xad sequent  analogous  problems  (e.g.,  Bassok & Holyoak,  1 989;  Gick & Holyoak,  1 983; Novick & Holyoak,  1 991 ;  Ross & Kennedy, 1 990). Research on  solution schemas is discussed in more detail by Holyoak (Chap. 6).\nI n  the remainder of  this section, we dis\xad cuss some of the recent research  on repre\xad sentation schemas (Hurley & Novick, 2004; Novick,  2001;  Novick,  Hurley, & Francis, 1 999). This research shows that college stu\xad dents  possess  abstract  schemas  for three spatial  diagrams matrices,  networks,  and hierarchies  that  are  important  tools  for understanding and solVing problems from a variety of domains  (see Tversky,  Chap.  10, for  a  general  review  of visuospatial  reason\xad ing).  These  schemas  presumably  were  in\xad duced over the course of students\' in-school and out-ofÔøΩschool experiences with concrete instances of these diagrams in  use (Novick, 2001 ) .   For  example,  matrices  are  used  for multiplication  tables,  time schedules,  grade books,  and  seating  charts.  The  spatial  dia\xad gram schemas seem to be at an intermedi\xad ate level of  generality (NOVick et aI., 1 999): Each type of  diagram is best suited for a par\xad ticular type of  relational structure, regardless of the  content domain in which  that struc\xad ture is embedded.  For example,  a matrix is appropriate whenever (I )  all possible com\xad binations  of items across two sets  must  be considered, (2) the relation between items is associative (i.e., nondirectional), and (3) it is important to be able to distinguish between items that are related and those that are not (NOVick & Hurley,  2001). The abstract rep\xad resentation schemas are more  useful than are specific  relevant example problems for un\xad derstanding the structures of  novel problems (NOVick et aI., 1 999).\nTo measure problem understanding, Novick et al.
